**Taiwan**,[^31] [^14] officially the **Republic of China** (**ROC**),[^32] is a country [^62] in [East Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia "East Asia").[^17] The main [island of Taiwan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Taiwan "Geography of Taiwan"), also known as *Formosa*, lies between the [East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_China_Sea "East China Sea") and [South China Seas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_China_Sea "South China Sea") in the northwestern [Pacific Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean "Pacific Ocean"), with the [People's Republic of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China") (PRC) to the northwest, [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japan "Japan") to the northeast, and the [Philippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippines "Philippines") to the south. It has an area of 35,808 square kilometers (13,826 square miles), with mountain ranges dominating the eastern two-thirds and plains in the western third, where its [highly urbanized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanization_by_country "Urbanization by country") population is concentrated. The combined [territories under ROC control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_area_of_the_Republic_of_China "Free area of the Republic of China") consist of [168 islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Taiwan "List of islands of Taiwan") [^18] in total covering 36,193 square kilometers (13,974 square miles).[^51] [^74] The [largest metropolitan area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taipei%E2%80%93Keelung_metropolitan_area "Taipei–Keelung metropolitan area") is formed by [Taipei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taipei "Taipei") (the capital), [New Taipei City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Taipei_City "New Taipei City"), and [Keelung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keelung "Keelung"). With around 23.9 million inhabitants, Taiwan is among the [most densely populated countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population_density "List of countries and dependencies by population density").

Taiwan has been settled for at least 25,000 years. Ancestors of [Taiwanese indigenous peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_indigenous_peoples "Taiwanese indigenous peoples") settled the island around 6,000 years ago. In the 17th century, large-scale [Han Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Han_Chinese "Han Chinese") immigration began under [Dutch colonial rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_Formosa "Dutch Formosa") and continued under the [Kingdom of Tungning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning "Kingdom of Tungning"), the first predominantly Han Chinese state in [Taiwanese history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Taiwan "History of Taiwan"). The island was [annexed in 1683](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule "Taiwan under Qing rule") by the [Qing dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qing_dynasty "Qing dynasty") and [ceded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki "Treaty of Shimonoseki") to the [Empire of Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_of_Japan "Empire of Japan") in 1895. The [Republic of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_China_\(1912%E2%80%931949\) "Republic of China (1912–1949)"), which had [overthrown the Qing in 1912](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1911_Revolution "1911 Revolution") under the leadership of [Sun Yat-sen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen "Sun Yat-sen"), assumed control following the [surrender of Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan "Surrender of Japan") in [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"). With the loss of [mainland China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_China "Mainland China") to the [Communists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China "Communist Party of China") in the [Chinese Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War "Chinese Civil War"), the [government of the Republic of China moved to Taiwan in 1949](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_the_government_of_the_Republic_of_China_to_Taiwan "Retreat of the government of the Republic of China to Taiwan") under the [Kuomintang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuomintang "Kuomintang") (KMT).

From the early 1960s, Taiwan saw rapid economic growth and industrialization known as the " [Taiwan Miracle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_Miracle "Taiwan Miracle") ".[^75] In the late 1980s and early 1990s, the ROC transitioned from a [one-party state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One-party_state "One-party state") under [martial law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law "Martial law") to a [multi-party democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-party_democracy "Multi-party democracy"), with democratically elected presidents beginning in [1996](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1996_Taiwanese_presidential_election "1996 Taiwanese presidential election"). Taiwan's [export-oriented economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Export-oriented_economy "Export-oriented economy") is the [21st-largest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\) "List of countries by GDP (nominal)") in the world by nominal GDP and the [20th-largest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(PPP\) "List of countries by GDP (PPP)") by PPP measures, with a focus on steel, machinery, electronics, and chemicals manufacturing. Taiwan is a [developed country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_country "Developed country").[^76] [^77] It is ranked highly in terms of [civil liberties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_liberties "Civil liberties"),[^78] [healthcare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Healthcare_in_Taiwan "Healthcare in Taiwan"),[^79] and [human development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_development_\(economics\) "Human development (economics)").[^19] [^83]

The [political](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_status_of_Taiwan "Political status of Taiwan") and [international status](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_status_of_Taiwan "International status of Taiwan") of Taiwan is contentious. Despite being a founding member, the ROC no longer represents China as a member of the [United Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations "United Nations") after UN members voted in 1971 to [recognize the PRC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_2758 "United Nations General Assembly Resolution 2758") instead. The ROC maintained its claim to be the sole [legitimate representative of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Two_Chinas "Two Chinas") and its territory until 1991, when it ceased to [regard the Chinese Communist Party as a rebellious group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporary_Provisions_against_the_Communist_Rebellion "Temporary Provisions against the Communist Rebellion") and acknowledged its control over mainland China. Taiwan is claimed by the PRC, which refuses to establish diplomatic relations with countries that recognize the ROC. Taiwan [maintains official diplomatic relations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Taiwan "Foreign relations of Taiwan") with 11 out of 193 UN member states and the [Holy See](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_See "Holy See"). Many others maintain unofficial diplomatic ties through [representative offices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taipei_Economic_and_Cultural_Representative_Office "Taipei Economic and Cultural Representative Office") and institutions that function as [*de facto* embassies and consulates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_facto_embassy#Taiwan "De facto embassy"). International organizations in which the PRC participates either refuse to grant membership to Taiwan or allow it to participate on a non-state basis. Domestically, the major political contention is between the [Pan-Blue Coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Blue_Coalition "Pan-Blue Coalition"), which favors eventual [Chinese unification under the ROC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_unification#Pan-Blue_interpretation "Chinese unification") and promoting a [pan-Chinese identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_nationalism "Chinese nationalism"), contrasted with the [Pan-Green Coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Green_Coalition "Pan-Green Coalition"), which favors eventual [Taiwan independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_independence_movement "Taiwan independence movement") and promoting a [Taiwanese identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_nationalism "Taiwanese nationalism"); in the 21st century, both sides have moderated their positions to broaden their appeal.[^85] [^86]

## Etymology

### Names for the island

In his *[Daoyi Zhilüe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daoyi_Zhil%C3%BCe "Daoyi Zhilüe")* (1349), [Wang Dayuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wang_Dayuan "Wang Dayuan") used " [Liuqiu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liuqiu_\(medieval\) "Liuqiu (medieval)") " as a name for the island, or the part of it closest to [Penghu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Penghu "Penghu").[^87] Elsewhere, the name was used for the [Ryukyu Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands "Ryukyu Islands") in general or [Okinawa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Okinawa_Island "Okinawa Island") specifically; the name *Ryūkyū* is the Japanese form of *Liúqiú*. The name also appears in the *[Book of Sui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Book_of_Sui "Book of Sui")* (636) and other early works, but scholars cannot agree on whether these references are to the Ryukyus, Taiwan or even [Luzon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luzon "Luzon").[^88]

The name *Formosa* ([福爾摩沙](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%A6%8F%E7%88%BE%E6%91%A9%E6%B2%99 "wikt:福爾摩沙")) dates from 1542, when Portuguese sailors noted it on their [maps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portolan_chart "Portolan chart") as *Ilha Formosa* ([Portuguese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portuguese_language "Portuguese language") for " *beautiful island* ").[^89] [^90] The name *Formosa* eventually "replaced all others in European literature" [^91] and remained in common use among English speakers into the 20th century.[^92]

In 1603, a Chinese expedition fleet anchored at a place in Taiwan called *Dayuan*, a variant of "Taiwan", which is derived from the [Siraya language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siraya_language "Siraya language").[^93] [^94] [^95] In the early 17th century, the [Dutch East India Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company "Dutch East India Company") established a commercial post at [Fort Zeelandia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fort_Zeelandia_\(Taiwan\) "Fort Zeelandia (Taiwan)") (modern-day [Anping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anping_District "Anping District")) on a coastal sandbar called "Tayouan".[^96] This name originated as an [ethnonym](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnonym "Ethnonym") for a nearby [Taiwanese indigenous people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_indigenous_people "Taiwanese indigenous people"),[^94] the [Taivoan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taivoan_people "Taivoan people"), who were historically classified as a subgroup of the Siraya.[^94] [^97]

Use of the current Chinese name ([臺灣](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3 "wikt:臺灣") / [台灣](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%8F%B0%E7%81%A3 "wikt:台灣")) became official as early as 1684 during the [Qing dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qing_dynasty "Qing dynasty") with the establishment of [Taiwan Prefecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_Prefecture "Taiwan Prefecture") centered in modern-day [Tainan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tainan "Tainan"). Through its rapid development the entire Taiwanese mainland eventually became known as "Taiwan".[^98] [^99] [^100] [^101]

### Names of the country and jurisdiction

The official name of the country in English is the "Republic of China". Shortly after the ROC's establishment in 1912, while it was still located on the Chinese mainland, the government used the short form "China" (**Zhōngguó**, [中國](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%AD%E5%9C%8B "wikt:中國")) to refer to itself, derived from *zhōng* ("central" or "middle") and *guó* ("state, nation-state").[^20] The term developed under the [Zhou dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhou_dynasty "Zhou dynasty") in reference to its [royal demesne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demesne "Demesne"),[^21] and was then applied to the area around Luoyi (present-day [Luoyang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luoyang "Luoyang")) during the [Eastern Zhou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Zhou "Eastern Zhou") and later to China's [Central Plain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Plain_\(China\) "Central Plain (China)"), before being used as an occasional synonym for the state during the Qing era.[^103] The name of the republic had stemmed from the party manifesto of the [Tongmenghui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tongmenghui "Tongmenghui") in 1905, which says the four goals of the Chinese revolution were "to expel the [Manchu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manchu_people "Manchu people") rulers, to revive [*Chunghwa*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Names_of_China#Zhongguo "Names of China"), to establish a Republic, and to distribute land equally among the people." [^33] Revolutionary leader [Sun Yat-sen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen "Sun Yat-sen") proposed the name *Chunghwa Minkuo* as the assumed name of the new country when the revolution succeeded.

During the 1950s and 1960s, after the ROC government had withdrawn to Taiwan, it was commonly referred to as "Nationalist China" (or " [Free China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_area_of_the_Republic_of_China "Free area of the Republic of China") ") to differentiate it from "communist China" (or " [Red China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China") ").[^105] Over subsequent decades, the Republic of China has become commonly known as "Taiwan", after the main island. To avoid confusion, the ROC government in Taiwan began to put "Taiwan" next to its official name in 2005.[^106] In ROC government publications, the name is written as "Republic of China (Taiwan)", "Republic of China/Taiwan", or sometimes "Taiwan (ROC)".[^107] [^108] [^109]

"Taiwan Area" was defined to mean the island of Taiwan, Penghu, Kinmen, Matsu, and other territory under ROC's effective control,[^110] in contrast to "Mainland Area" which refers to ROC territory outside the Taiwan Area and under Chinese Communist control.[^111]

The Republic of China participates in most international forums and organizations under the name " [Chinese Taipei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Taipei "Chinese Taipei") " as a compromise with the People's Republic of China (PRC). For instance, it is the name under which it has participated in the [Olympic Games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olympic_Games "Olympic Games") as well as the [APEC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation "Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation").[^112] "Taiwan authorities" is sometimes used by the PRC to refer to the government in Taiwan.[^113]

## History

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/%E5%8D%91%E5%8D%97%E9%81%BA%E5%9D%80%E4%BA%BA%E7%8D%B8%E5%BD%A2%E7%8E%89%E7%8E%A6.jpg/250px-%E5%8D%91%E5%8D%97%E9%81%BA%E5%9D%80%E4%BA%BA%E7%8D%B8%E5%BD%A2%E7%8E%89%E7%8E%A6.jpg)

2,300-year-old jade, unearthed at Beinan Cultural Park

### Pre-colonial period

Taiwan was joined to the Asian mainland in the [Late Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Pleistocene "Late Pleistocene"), until sea levels rose about 10,000 years ago.[^114] Human remains and [Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleolithic "Paleolithic") artifacts dated 20,000 to 30,000 years ago have been found.[^115] [^116] Study of the human remains suggested they were [Australo-Papuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australo-Papuan "Australo-Papuan") people similar to [Negrito populations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negrito "Negrito") in the Philippines.[^117] Paleolithic Taiwanese likely settled the [Ryukyu Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands "Ryukyu Islands") 30,000 years ago [^118] although other studies suggest no genetic contribution from the aborigines of Taiwan into Ryukyuans.[^119] [^120] [^121] [^122] [Slash-and-burn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slash-and-burn "Slash-and-burn") agriculture practices started at least 11,000 years ago.[^123]

Stone tools of the [Changbin culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Changbin_culture "Changbin culture") have been found in [Taitung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taitung_County "Taitung County") and [Eluanbi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eluanbi "Eluanbi"). Archaeological remains suggest they were initially hunter-gatherers that slowly shifted to intensive fishing.[^124] [^125] The distinct [Wangxing culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wangxing_culture "Wangxing culture"), found in [Miaoli County](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miaoli_County "Miaoli County"), seemed to have initially been gatherers who shifted to hunting.[^126] [^127]

Around 6,000 years ago, Taiwan was settled by farmers of the [Dapenkeng culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dapenkeng_culture "Dapenkeng culture"), most likely from what is now southeast China.[^128] These cultures are the ancestors of modern [Taiwanese Indigenous peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_indigenous_peoples "Taiwanese indigenous peoples") and the [originators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linguistic_homeland "Linguistic homeland") of the [Austronesian language family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austronesian_language_family "Austronesian language family").[^129] [^130] Trade with the [Philippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippines "Philippines") persisted from the early 2nd millennium BCE, including the use of Taiwanese [jade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jade "Jade") in the Philippine jade culture.[^131] [^132]

The Dapenkeng culture was succeeded by a variety of cultures throughout the island, including the [Tahu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tahu_culture "Tahu culture") and [Yingpu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yingpu_culture "Yingpu culture"); the Yuanshan were characterized by rice harvesting. Iron appeared in such cultures as the [Niaosung culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niaosung_culture "Niaosung culture"), influenced by trade with China and [Maritime Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia "Maritime Southeast Asia").[^133] [^134] The [Plains Indigenous peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plains_Indigenous_peoples "Plains Indigenous peoples") mainly lived in permanent walled villages, with a lifestyle based on [agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture"), [fishing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fishing "Fishing"), and [hunting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hunting "Hunting").[^135] They had traditionally [matriarchal societies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matriarchal_society "Matriarchal society").[^135]

### Early colonial period (to 1683)

The [Penghu Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Penghu "Penghu") were inhabited by [Han Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Han_Chinese "Han Chinese") fishermen by 1171, and in 1225 Penghu was attached to [Jinjiang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinjiang,_Fujian "Jinjiang, Fujian").[^136] [^137] [^138] [^139] The [Yuan dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuan_dynasty "Yuan dynasty") officially incorporated Penghu under the jurisdiction of [Tong'an](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tong%27an "Tong'an") County in 1281.[^139] Penghu was evacuated in the 15th century by the [Ming dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ming_dynasty "Ming dynasty") as part of their [maritime ban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haijin "Haijin"), which lasted until the late 16th century.[^140] In 1349, [Wang Dayuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wang_Dayuan "Wang Dayuan") provided the first written account of a visit to Taiwan.[^141] [^142] By the 1590s, a small number of Chinese from [Fujian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fujian "Fujian") had started cultivating land in southwestern Taiwan.[^143] Some 1,500-2,000 Chinese lived or stayed temporarily on the southern coast of Taiwan, mostly for seasonal fishing but also subsistence farming and trading, by the early 17th century.[^144] [^142] In 1603, [Chen Di](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chen_Di "Chen Di") visited Taiwan on an anti- [wokou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wokou "Wokou") expedition and recorded an account of the Taiwanese Indigenous people.[^94]

In 1591, Japan sent envoys to deliver a letter requesting tribute relations with Taiwan. They found no leader to deliver the letter to and returned home. In 1609, a Japanese expedition was sent to survey Taiwan. After being attacked by the Indigenous people, they took some prisoners and returned home. In 1616, a Japanese fleet of 13 ships were sent to Taiwan. Due to a storm, only one ship made it there and is presumed to have returned to Japan.[^145] [^146]

![Photograph of a European style fortification with stone walls and a white pointed tower.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Tainan_Taiwan_Fort-Zeelandia-01.jpg/250px-Tainan_Taiwan_Fort-Zeelandia-01.jpg)

Fort Zeelandia, built in 1634, was the governor 's residence in Dutch Formosa.

In 1624, the [Dutch East India Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company "Dutch East India Company") (VOC) established [Fort Zeelandia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fort_Zeelandia_\(Taiwan\) "Fort Zeelandia (Taiwan)") on the coastal islet of Tayouan (in modern [Tainan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tainan "Tainan")).[^147] [^101] The lowland areas were occupied by 11 Indigenous [chiefdoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiefdom "Chiefdom"), some of which fell under Dutch control, including the [Kingdom of Middag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Middag "Kingdom of Middag").[^101] [^148] When the Dutch arrived, southwestern Taiwan was already frequented by a mostly transient Chinese population numbering close to 1,500.[^144] The VOC encouraged Chinese farmers to immigrate and work the lands under Dutch control and by the 1660s, some 30,000 to 50,000 Chinese were living on the island.[^149] [^150] Most of the farmers cultivated rice for local consumption and sugar for export while some immigrants engaged in deer hunting for export.[^151] [^152] [^153]

In 1626, the [Spanish Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_Empire "Spanish Empire") occupied northern Taiwan as a trading base, first at [Keelung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keelung "Keelung") and in 1628 building [Fort Santo Domingo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fort_Santo_Domingo "Fort Santo Domingo") at [Tamsui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tamsui "Tamsui").[^154] [^155] This colony lasted until 1642, when the last Spanish fortress fell to Dutch forces.[^156] The Dutch then marched south, subduing hundreds of villages in the western plains.[^156]

![Photo of an elaborate Chinese temple with hedges in front.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Tainan_Taiwan_Confucius-Temple-06.jpg/250px-Tainan_Taiwan_Confucius-Temple-06.jpg)

Tainan Confucian Temple built in 1665 during the Kingdom of Tungning period

Following the fall of the Ming dynasty in Beijing in 1644, [Koxinga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koxinga "Koxinga") (Zheng Chenggong) pledged allegiance to the [Yongli Emperor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yongli_Emperor "Yongli Emperor") and attacked the Qing dynasty along the southeastern coast of China.[^157] In 1661, under increasing Qing pressure, he moved his forces from his base in [Xiamen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xiamen "Xiamen") to Taiwan, [expelling the Dutch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Fort_Zeelandia "Siege of Fort Zeelandia") the following year. The Dutch retook the northern fortress at Keelung in 1664, but left the island in 1668 in the face of indigenous resistance.[^158] [^159]

The Zheng regime, known as the [Kingdom of Tungning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning "Kingdom of Tungning"), proclaimed its loyalty to the overthrown Ming, but ruled independently.[^160] [^161] [^162] [^163] However, [Zheng Jing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zheng_Jing "Zheng Jing") 's return to China to participate in the [Revolt of the Three Feudatories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Three_Feudatories "Revolt of the Three Feudatories") paved the way for the Qing invasion and occupation of Taiwan in 1683.[^164] [^165]

### Qing rule (1683–1895)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tainan_Taiwan_Fort-Provintia-01.jpg/250px-Tainan_Taiwan_Fort-Provintia-01.jpg)

Chihkan Tower, originally built as Fort Provintia by the Dutch, was rebuilt under Qing rule.

Following the defeat of [Koxinga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koxinga "Koxinga") 's grandson by an armada led by Admiral [Shi Lang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shi_Lang "Shi Lang") in 1683, the Qing dynasty formally annexed Taiwan in May 1684, making it a [prefecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_prefecture "Taiwan prefecture") of Fujian province while retaining its administrative seat (now Tainan) under Koxinga as the capital.[^166] [^167] [^168]

The Qing government generally tried to restrict migration to Taiwan throughout the duration of its administration because it believed that Taiwan could not sustain too large a population without leading to conflict. After the defeat of the Kingdom of Tungning, most of its population in Taiwan was sent back to the mainland, leaving the official population count at only 50,000, including 10,000 troops. Despite official restrictions, officials in Taiwan solicited settlers from the mainland, causing tens of thousands of annual arrivals by 1711. A permit system was officially recorded in 1712, but it likely existed as early as 1684; its restrictions included only allowing those to enter who had property on the mainland, family in Taiwan, and who were not accompanied by wives or children. Many of the male migrants married local Indigenous women. Over the 18th century, restrictions were relaxed. In 1732, families were allowed to move to Taiwan.[^169] [^170] By 1811, there were more than two million Han settlers in Taiwan, and profitable sugar and rice production industries provided exports to the mainland.[^171] [^172] [^173] In 1875, restrictions on entering Taiwan were repealed.[^174]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Taiwanese_aboriginese_deerhunt1.png/250px-Taiwanese_aboriginese_deerhunt1.png)

Taiwanese indigenous peoples hunting deer, 1746

Three counties nominally covered the entire western plains, but actual control was restricted to a smaller area. A government permit was required for settlers to go beyond the [Dajia River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dajia_River "Dajia River"). Qing administration expanded across the western plains area over the 18th century due to continued illegal crossings and settlement.[^175] The Taiwanese Indigenous peoples were categorized by the Qing administration into acculturated aborigines who had adopted Han culture and non-acculturated aborigines who had not. The Qing did little to administer or subjugate them. When Taiwan was annexed, there were 46 aboriginal villages under its control, likely inherited from the Kingdom of Tungning. During the early [Qianlong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor "Qianlong Emperor") period there were 93 acculturated villages and 61 non-acculturated villages that paid taxes. In response to the [Zhu Yigui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhu_Yigui "Zhu Yigui") settler rebellion in 1722, separation of aboriginals and settlers became official policy via 54 stelae used to mark the frontier boundary. The markings were changed four times over the latter half of the 18th century due to continued settler encroachment. Two aboriginal affairs sub-prefects, one for the north and one for the south, were appointed in 1766.[^176]

During the 200 years of Qing rule in Taiwan, the [Plains Indigenous peoples](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plains_Indigenous_peoples "Plains Indigenous peoples") rarely rebelled against the government and the mountain Indigenous peoples were left to their own devices until the last 20 years of Qing rule. Most of the more than 100 rebellions during the Qing period, such as the [Lin Shuangwen rebellion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lin_Shuangwen_rebellion "Lin Shuangwen rebellion"), were caused by Han settlers.[^177] [^178] Their frequency was evoked by the common saying "every three years an uprising, every five years a rebellion" (三年一反、五年一亂), primarily in reference to the period between 1820 and 1850.[^179] [^180] [^181]

Many officials stationed in Taiwan called for an active colonization policy over the 19th century. In 1788, Taiwan Prefect Yang Tingli supported the efforts of a settler named Wu Sha to claim land held by the [Kavalan people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kavalan_people "Kavalan people"). In 1797, Wu Sha was able to recruit settlers with financial support from the local government but was unable to officially register the land. In the early 1800s, local officials convinced the emperor to officially incorporate the area by playing up the issue of piracy if the land was left alone.[^182] In 1814, some settlers attempted to colonize central Taiwan by fabricating rights to lease aboriginal land. They were evicted by government troops two years later. Local officials continued to advocate for the colonization of the area but were ignored.[^183]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/TW_%E5%8F%B0%E7%81%A3_Taiwan_TPE_%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E5%B8%82_Taipei_City_%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E5%8D%80_Zhongzheng_District_%E5%BF%A0%E5%AD%9D%E8%A5%BF%E8%B7%AF_Zhongxiao_West_Road_%E6%89%BF%E6%81%A9%E9%96%80_%E8%87%BA%E5%8C%97%E5%BA%9C%E5%9F%8E%E5%8C%97%E9%96%80_morning_August_2019_IX2_06.jpg/250px-thumbnail.jpg)

Taipei North Gate, constructed in 1884, was part of the Walls of Taipei.

The Qing took on a more active colonization policy after 1874 when Japan [invaded Indigenous territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mudan_Incident "Mudan Incident") in southern Taiwan and the Qing government was forced to pay an indemnity for them to leave.[^184] The administration of Taiwan was expanded with new prefectures, sub-prefectures, and counties. Mountain roads were constructed to make inner Taiwan more accessible. Restrictions on entering Taiwan were ended in 1875 and agencies for recruiting settlers were established on the mainland, but efforts to promote settlement ended soon after.[^185] In 1884, [Keelung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keelung "Keelung") in northern Taiwan was occupied during the [Sino-French War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-French_War "Sino-French War") but the French forces failed to advance any further inland while their victory at Penghu in 1885 resulted in disease and retreat soon afterward as the war ended. Colonization efforts were renewed under [Liu Mingchuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liu_Mingchuan "Liu Mingchuan"). In 1887, Taiwan's status was upgraded to a [province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_\(Republic_of_China_province\) "Taiwan (Republic of China province)"). [Taipei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taipei "Taipei") became the permanent capital in 1893. Liu's efforts to increase revenues on Taiwan's produce were hampered by foreign pressure not to increase levies. A land reform was implemented, increasing revenue which still fell short of expectation.[^186] [^187] [^188] Modern technologies such as electric lighting, a railway, telegraph lines, steamship service, and industrial machinery were introduced under Liu's governance, but several of these projects had mixed results. A campaign to formally subjugate the Indigenous peoples ended with the loss of a third of the army after fierce resistance from the Mkgogan and Msbtunux peoples. Liu resigned in 1891 due to criticism of these costly projects.[^189] [^190] [^166] [^191]

By the end of the Qing period, the western plains were fully developed as farmland with about 2.5 million Chinese settlers. The mountainous areas were still largely autonomous under the control of Indigenous peoples. Indigenous land loss under the Qing occurred at a relatively slow pace due to the absence of state-sponsored land deprivation for the majority of Qing rule.[^192] [^193]

### Japanese rule (1895–1945)

Following the Qing defeat in the [First Sino-Japanese War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War "First Sino-Japanese War") (1894–1895), Taiwan, its associated islands, and the Penghu archipelago were ceded to [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empire_of_Japan "Empire of Japan") by the [Treaty of Shimonoseki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki "Treaty of Shimonoseki").[^194] Inhabitants wishing to remain Qing subjects had to move to mainland China within a two-year grace period, which few saw as feasible.[^195] Estimates say around 4,000 to 6,000 departed before the expiration of the grace period, and 200,000 to 300,000 followed during the subsequent disorder.[^196] [^172] [^197] On 25 May 1895, a group of pro-Qing high officials proclaimed the [Republic of Formosa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Formosa "Republic of Formosa") to resist impending Japanese rule. Japanese forces entered the capital at Tainan and quelled this resistance on 21 October 1895.[^198] About 6,000 inhabitants died in the initial fighting and some 14,000 died in the first year of Japanese rule. Another 12,000 "bandit-rebels" were killed from 1898 to 1902.[^199] [^200] [^201] Subsequent rebellions against the Japanese (the [Beipu uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beipu_uprising "Beipu uprising") of 1907, the [Tapani incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tapani_incident "Tapani incident") of 1915, and the [Musha incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wushe_incident "Wushe incident") of 1930) were unsuccessful but demonstrated opposition to Japanese rule.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Taiwan_Seito_Wanli_Factory_1930s.jpg/250px-Taiwan_Seito_Wanli_Factory_1930s.jpg)

A sugarcane mill and its railways in Tainan in the 1930s

The colonial period was instrumental to the industrialization of the island, with its expansion of railways and other transport networks, the building of an extensive sanitation system, the establishment of a formal [education system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_Taiwan "Education in Taiwan"), and an end to the practice of [headhunting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Headhunting "Headhunting").[^202] [^203] The resources of Taiwan were used to aid the development of Japan. The production of [cash crops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cash_crops "Cash crops") such as sugar greatly increased, and large areas were therefore diverted from the production of rice.[^204] By 1939, Taiwan was the seventh-greatest sugar producer in the world.[^205]

The Han and Indigenous populations were classified as second- and third-class citizens, and many prestigious government and business positions were closed to them.[^206] After suppressing Han guerrillas in the first decade of their rule, Japanese authorities engaged in bloody campaigns against the Indigenous people residing in mountainous regions, culminating in the Musha Incident of 1930.[^207] Intellectuals and laborers who participated in left-wing movements were also arrested and massacred (e.g. [Chiang Wei-shui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiang_Wei-shui "Chiang Wei-shui") and [Masanosuke Watanabe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masanosuke_Watanabe "Masanosuke Watanabe")).[^208] Around 1936, the Japanese governor-general [Seizō Kobayashi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seiz%C5%8D_Kobayashi "Seizō Kobayashi") began an island-wide [assimilation project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanization "Japanization").[^209] Chinese-language newspapers were abolished. National [Shinto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Shinto "State Shinto") religion was promoted in parallel with suppression of traditional Taiwanese beliefs. By late 1940, when temple destruction in Taiwan had ended, the number of native temples had decreased by a third. Starting from 1940, families were also encouraged to adopt [Japanese surnames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_surname "Japanese surname") (a policy called [kaiseimei](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Kaiseimei&action=edit&redlink=1 "Kaiseimei (page does not exist)")), although only slightly more than 2% had done so by 1943, and possibly about 7% by August 1945.[^209] By 1938, 309,000 Japanese were residing in Taiwan.[^210] During the Second World War, the island was developed into a naval and air base while its agriculture, industry, and commerce suffered.[^211] [^212] Air attacks and the subsequent invasion of the [Philippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippines "Philippines") were launched from Taiwan. The [Imperial Japanese Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy "Imperial Japanese Navy") operated heavily from Taiwanese ports, and its think tank " [South Strike Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanshin-ron#Theoretical_development "Nanshin-ron") " was based at [Taihoku Imperial University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taihoku_Imperial_University "Taihoku Imperial University"). Military bases and industrial centers, such as [Kaohsiung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaohsiung "Kaohsiung") and [Keelung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keelung "Keelung"), became targets of heavy [Allied bombings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raid_on_Taipei "Raid on Taipei"), which destroyed many of the factories, dams, and transport facilities built by the Japanese.[^213] [^212] In October 1944, the [Formosa Air Battle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formosa_Air_Battle "Formosa Air Battle") was fought between American carriers and Japanese forces in Taiwan. Over 200,000 [Taiwanese served in the Japanese military](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_Imperial_Japan_Serviceman "Taiwanese Imperial Japan Serviceman"), with over 30,000 casualties.[^214] Over 2,000 women, euphemistically called " [comfort women](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comfort_women "Comfort women") ", were forced into sexual slavery for Imperial Japanese troops.[^215]

After [Japan's surrender](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japan%27s_surrender "Japan's surrender"), most Japanese residents were [expelled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II_evacuation_and_expulsion "World War II evacuation and expulsion").[^216]

### Republic of China (1945–present)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/General_Chen_Yi_of_China_accepts_the_surrender_of_And%C5%8D_Rikichi%2C_the_Japanese_Governor-General_of.jpg/250px-General_Chen_Yi_of_China_accepts_the_surrender_of_And%C5%8D_Rikichi%2C_the_Japanese_Governor-General_of.jpg)

General Chen Yi (right) accepting the receipt of General Order No. 1 from Rikichi Andō (left), the last Japanese governor-general of Taiwan, in Taipei City Hall

While Taiwan was under Japanese rule, the [Republic of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_China_\(1912%E2%80%931949\) "Republic of China (1912–1949)") was founded on [mainland China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_China "Mainland China") on 1 January 1912 following the [Xinhai Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution "Xinhai Revolution") of 1911.[^217] Central authority waxed and waned in response to [warlordism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warlord_Era "Warlord Era") (1915–28), [Japanese invasion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War "Second Sino-Japanese War") (1937–45), and the [Chinese Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War "Chinese Civil War") (1927–49), with central authority strongest during the [Nanjing decade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanjing_decade "Nanjing decade") (1927–37), when most of China came under the control of the [Kuomintang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuomintang "Kuomintang") (KMT).[^218] During [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"), the 1943 [Cairo Declaration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1943_Cairo_Declaration "1943 Cairo Declaration") specified that Formosa and the Pescadores be returned by Japan to the ROC;[^219] [^220] the terms were later repeated in the 1945 [Potsdam Declaration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration "Potsdam Declaration") [^221] that Japan agreed to carry out in [its instrument of surrender](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Instrument_of_Surrender "Japanese Instrument of Surrender").[^222] [^223] On [25 October 1945](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retrocession_Day "Retrocession Day"), Japan surrendered Taiwan to the ROC, and in the [Treaty of San Francisco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Francisco "Treaty of San Francisco"), Japan formally renounced their claims to the islands, though without specifying to whom they were surrendered.[^224] [^225] [^226] [^227] [^22] In 1952, Japan and the ROC signed [a peace treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Taipei "Treaty of Taipei").[^228]

While initially enthusiastic about the return of Chinese administration and the [Three Principles of the People](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three_Principles_of_the_People "Three Principles of the People"), Formosans grew increasingly dissatisfied about being excluded from higher positions, the postponement of local elections even after the enactment of a [constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ROC_Constitution "ROC Constitution") on the mainland, the smuggling of valuables off the island, the expropriation of businesses into government-operated monopolies, and the [hyperinflation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperinflation "Hyperinflation") of 1945–1949.[^229] [^230] [^231] [^232] The shooting of a civilian on 28 February 1947 triggered island-wide unrest, which was suppressed with military force in what is now called the [February 28 incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/February_28_incident "February 28 incident").[^233] [^234] Mainstream estimates of the number killed range from 18,000 to 30,000.[^235] [^236] [^237] Chen was later replaced by [Wei Tao-ming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wei_Tao-ming "Wei Tao-ming"), who made an effort to undo previous mismanagement by re-appointing a good proportion of islanders and re-privatizing businesses.[^238]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ROC_Retreat_to_Taiwan.svg/250px-ROC_Retreat_to_Taiwan.svg.png)

The Nationalists' retreat to Taipei

After the end of World War II, the Chinese Civil War resumed. A series of Chinese Communist offensives in 1949 led to the capture of its capital [Nanjing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanjing "Nanjing") on 23 April and the subsequent defeat of the Nationalists on the mainland. The Communists [founded the People's Republic of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China "Proclamation of the People's Republic of China") on 1 October.[^239] On 7 December 1949, [Chiang Kai-Shek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiang_Kai-Shek "Chiang Kai-Shek") [evacuated his Nationalist government to Taiwan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retreat_of_the_Republic_of_China_to_Taiwan "Retreat of the Republic of China to Taiwan") and made Taipei the [temporary capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporary_capital "Temporary capital") of the ROC.[^240] Some 2 million people, mainly soldiers, members of the ruling Kuomintang and intellectual and business elites, were evacuated to Taiwan, adding to the earlier population of approximately six million. These people and their descendants became known in Taiwan as *[waishengren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Waishengren "Waishengren")* (外省人). The ROC government took to Taipei many national treasures and much of China's [gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold_reserves "Gold reserves") and foreign currency reserves.[^241] [^242] [^243] Most of the gold was used to pay soldiers' salaries,[^244] with some used to issue the [New Taiwan dollar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Taiwan_dollar "New Taiwan dollar"), part of a price stabilization program to slow inflation in Taiwan.[^245] [^246]

After losing control of mainland China in 1949, the ROC retained control of Taiwan and Penghu ([Taiwan, ROC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_\(Republic_of_China_province\) "Taiwan (Republic of China province)")), parts of Fujian ([Fujian, ROC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fujian_Province,_Republic_of_China "Fujian Province, Republic of China"))—specifically Kinmen, [Wuqiu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wuqiu,_Kinmen "Wuqiu, Kinmen") (now part of Kinmen) and the Matsu Islands and two major [islands in the South China Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_China_Sea_Islands "South China Sea Islands"). The ROC also briefly retained control of the entirety of [Hainan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hainan "Hainan"), parts of [Zhejiang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zhejiang "Zhejiang") ([Chekiang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chekiang_Province,_Republic_of_China "Chekiang Province, Republic of China"))—specifically the [Dachen Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dachen_Islands "Dachen Islands") and [Yijiangshan Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yijiangshan_Islands "Yijiangshan Islands") —and portions of [Tibet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tibet_\(1912%E2%80%931951\) "Tibet (1912–1951)"), [Qinghai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qinghai "Qinghai"), [Xinjiang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xinjiang_Province,_Republic_of_China "Xinjiang Province, Republic of China") and [Yunnan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yunnan "Yunnan"). The Communists [captured Hainan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Hainan_Island "Battle of Hainan Island") in 1950, captured the Dachen Islands and Yijiangshan Islands during the [First Taiwan Strait Crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Taiwan_Strait_Crisis "First Taiwan Strait Crisis") in 1955 and defeated the [ROC revolts in Northwest China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuomintang_Islamic_insurgency "Kuomintang Islamic insurgency") in 1958. ROC forces entered Burma and Thailand in the 1950s and [were defeated by Communists in 1961](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1960%E2%80%9361_campaign_at_the_China%E2%80%93Burma_border "1960–61 campaign at the China–Burma border"). Since losing control of mainland China, the Kuomintang continued to claim sovereignty over 'all of China', which it defined to include mainland China (including Tibet), Taiwan (including Penghu), [Outer Mongolia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Mongolia "Outer Mongolia"), and [other minor territories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Taiwan "Administrative divisions of Taiwan").

#### Martial law era (1949–1987)

![A Chinese man in military uniform, smiling and looking towards the left. He holds a sword in his left hand and has a medal in shape of a sun on his chest.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Chiang_Kai-shek_in_full_uniform.jpeg/250px-Chiang_Kai-shek_in_full_uniform.jpeg)

Chiang Kai-shek, leader of the Kuomintang from 1925 until his death in 1975

[Martial law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law "Martial law"), declared on Taiwan in May 1949,[^247] continued to be in effect until 1987,[^247] [^248] and was used to suppress political opposition. During the [White Terror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_Terror_\(Taiwan\) "White Terror (Taiwan)"), as the period is known, 140,000 people were imprisoned or executed for being perceived as anti-KMT or pro-Communist.[^249] Many citizens were arrested, tortured, imprisoned or executed for their real or perceived link to the Chinese Communist Party. Since these people were mainly from the intellectual and social elite, an entire generation of political and social leaders was destroyed.

Following the eruption of the [Korean War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korean_War "Korean War"), US President [Harry S. Truman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harry_S._Truman "Harry S. Truman") dispatched the [United States Seventh Fleet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Seventh_Fleet "United States Seventh Fleet") into the [Taiwan Strait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_Strait "Taiwan Strait") to prevent hostilities between the ROC and the PRC.[^250] The United States also passed the [Sino-American Mutual Defense Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-American_Mutual_Defense_Treaty "Sino-American Mutual Defense Treaty") and the [Formosa Resolution of 1955](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formosa_Resolution_of_1955 "Formosa Resolution of 1955"), granting substantial [foreign aid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_foreign_aid "United States foreign aid") to the KMT regime between 1951 and 1965.[^251] The US foreign aid stabilized prices in Taiwan by 1952.[^252] The KMT government instituted many laws and [land reforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_reform_in_Taiwan "Land reform in Taiwan") that it had never effectively enacted on mainland China.[^253] Economic development was encouraged by American aid and programs such as the [Joint Commission on Rural Reconstruction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-American_Joint_Commission_on_Rural_Reconstruction "Sino-American Joint Commission on Rural Reconstruction"), which turned the agricultural sector into the basis for later growth. Under the combined stimulus of the land reform and the agricultural development programs, agricultural production increased at an average annual rate of 4 percent from 1952 to 1959.[^254] The government also implemented a policy of [import substitution industrialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Import_substitution_industrialization "Import substitution industrialization"), attempting to produce imported goods domestically.[^255] The policy promoted the development of textile, food, and other labor-intensive industries.[^256]

As the Chinese Civil War continued, the government built up military fortifications throughout Taiwan. Veterans built the [Central Cross-Island Highway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Cross-Island_Highway "Central Cross-Island Highway") through the [Taroko Gorge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taroko_Gorge "Taroko Gorge") in the 1950s. During the [Second Taiwan Strait Crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Taiwan_Strait_Crisis "Second Taiwan Strait Crisis") in 1958, [Nike Hercules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nike_Hercules "Nike Hercules") missiles were added to the formation of missile batteries throughout the island.[^257] [^258]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/250px-U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg)

With Chiang Kai-shek, US president Dwight D. Eisenhower waved to crowds during his visit to Taipei in June 1960.

During the 1960s and 1970s, the ROC maintained an authoritarian, single-party government under the Kuomintang's [Dang Guo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dang_Guo "Dang Guo") system while its economy became industrialized and technology-oriented.[^259] This rapid economic growth, known as the [Taiwan Miracle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_Miracle "Taiwan Miracle"), occurred following a strategy of prioritizing agriculture, light industries, and heavy industries, in that order.[^260] [Export-oriented industrialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Export-oriented_industrialization "Export-oriented industrialization") was achieved by tax rebate for exports, removal of import restriction, moving from multiple exchange rate to single exchange rate system, and depreciation of the New Taiwan dollar.[^261] [Infrastructure projects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ten_Major_Construction_Projects "Ten Major Construction Projects") such as the [Sun Yat-sen Freeway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen_Freeway "Sun Yat-sen Freeway"), [Taoyuan International Airport](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taoyuan_International_Airport "Taoyuan International Airport"), [Taichung Harbor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taichung_Harbor "Taichung Harbor"), and [Jinshan Nuclear Power Plant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinshan_Nuclear_Power_Plant "Jinshan Nuclear Power Plant") were launched, while the rise of steel, petrochemical, and shipbuilding industries in southern Taiwan saw the transformation of Kaohsiung into a special municipality on par with Taipei.[^262] In the 1970s, Taiwan became the second fastest growing economy in Asia.[^263] Real growth in [GDP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") averaged over 10 percent.[^264] In 1978, the combination of tax incentives and a cheap, well-trained labor force attracted investments of over $1.9 billion from [overseas Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overseas_Chinese "Overseas Chinese"), the United States, and Japan.[^265] By 1980, foreign trade reached $39 billion per year and generated a surplus of $46.5 million.[^260] Along with Hong Kong, Singapore, and South Korea, Taiwan became known as one of the [Four Asian Tigers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers "Four Asian Tigers").

Because of the Cold War, most Western nations and the United Nations regarded the ROC as the sole legitimate government of China until the 1970s. Eventually, especially after [Taiwan's expulsion from the United Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_2758 "United Nations General Assembly Resolution 2758"), most nations switched [diplomatic recognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition "Diplomatic recognition") to the PRC. Until the 1970s, the ROC government was regarded by Western critics as undemocratic for upholding martial law, severely repressing any political opposition, and controlling the media. The KMT did not allow the creation of new parties and competitive democratic elections did not exist.[^266] [^267] [^268] [^269] [^270]

From the late 1970s to the 1990s, Taiwan underwent political and social reforms that transformed it into a democracy.[^271] [^272] [Chiang Ching-kuo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiang_Ching-kuo "Chiang Ching-kuo"), Chiang Kai-shek's son, served as [premier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Premier_of_the_Republic_of_China "Premier of the Republic of China") from 1972 and rose to the presidency in 1978. He sought to move more authority to " [bensheng ren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bensheng_ren "Bensheng ren") " (residents of Taiwan before Japan's surrender and their descendants).[^273] Pro-democracy activists *[Tangwai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangwai_movement "Tangwai movement")* emerged as the opposition. In 1979, the [Kaohsiung Incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaohsiung_Incident "Kaohsiung Incident") took place in [Kaohsiung](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaohsiung "Kaohsiung") on [Human Rights Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Day "Human Rights Day"). Although the protest was rapidly crushed by the authorities, it is considered as the main event that united Taiwan's opposition.[^274]

In 1984, Chiang Ching-kuo selected [Lee Teng-hui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui "Lee Teng-hui") as his vice-president. After the [Democratic Progressive Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Progressive_Party "Democratic Progressive Party") (DPP) was (illegally) founded as the first opposition party in Taiwan to counter the KMT in 1986, Chiang announced that he would allow the formation of new parties.[^275] On 15 July 1987, Chiang lifted martial law on the main island of Taiwan.[^276] [^277]

#### Transition to democracy

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/President_Lee_teng_hui.png/250px-President_Lee_teng_hui.png)

In 1988, Lee Teng-hui became the first president of the Republic of China born in Taiwan and was the first to be directly elected in 1996.

After Chiang Ching-kuo's death in 1988, [Lee Teng-hui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lee_Teng-hui "Lee Teng-hui") became the first president of the ROC born in Taiwan.[^278] Lee's administration oversaw a period of [democratization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratization "Democratization") in which the [Temporary Provisions against the Communist Rebellion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporary_Provisions_against_the_Communist_Rebellion "Temporary Provisions against the Communist Rebellion") were abolished and the [Additional Articles of the Constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additional_Articles_of_the_Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_China "Additional Articles of the Constitution of the Republic of China") were introduced.[^279] [^280] Congressional representation was allocated to only the [Taiwan Area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_area_of_the_Republic_of_China "Free area of the Republic of China"),[^281] and Taiwan underwent a process of [localization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwanese_nationalism "Taiwanese nationalism") in which Taiwanese culture and history were promoted over a [pan-China viewpoint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_nationalism#In_Taiwan "Chinese nationalism") [^282] while [assimilationist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_assimilation "Cultural assimilation") policies were replaced with support for [multiculturalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiculturalism "Multiculturalism").[^283] In 1996, Lee was re-elected in [the first direct presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1996_Taiwanese_presidential_election "1996 Taiwanese presidential election").[^284] During Lee's administration, both he and his party were involved in corruption controversies that came to be known as " [black gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_gold_\(politics\) "Black gold (politics)") " politics.[^285] [^286] [^287]

[Chen Shui-bian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chen_Shui-bian "Chen Shui-bian") of the DPP was [elected as the first non-KMT president in 2000](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2000_Taiwanese_presidential_election "2000 Taiwanese presidential election").[^288] However, Chen lacked legislative majority. The opposition KMT developed the [Pan-Blue Coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Blue_Coalition "Pan-Blue Coalition") with other parties, mustering a slim majority over the DPP-led [Pan-Green Coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-Green_Coalition "Pan-Green Coalition").[^289] Polarized politics emerged in Taiwan with the Pan-Blue preference for eventual [Chinese unification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_unification "Chinese unification"), while the Pan-Green prefers [Taiwanese independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_independence_movement "Taiwan independence movement").

Chen's reference to " [One Country on Each Side](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One_Country_on_Each_Side "One Country on Each Side") " of the Taiwan Strait undercut [cross-Strait relations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross-Strait_relations "Cross-Strait relations") in 2002.[^290] He pushed for the [first national referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2004_Taiwanese_cross-Strait_relations_referendum "2004 Taiwanese cross-Strait relations referendum") on cross-Strait relations,[^291] [^292] and called for an end to the [National Unification Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Unification_Council "National Unification Council").[^293] State-run companies began dropping "China" references in their names and including "Taiwan".[^294] In 2008, [referendums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_Taiwanese_United_Nations_membership_referendums "2008 Taiwanese United Nations membership referendums") asked whether Taiwan should join the UN.[^295] This act alienated moderate constituents who supported the status quo, as well as those with cross-strait economic ties. It also created tension with the mainland and disagreements with the United States.[^296] Chen's administration was also dogged by public concerns over reduced economic growth, legislative gridlock, and [corruption investigations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chen_Shui-bian_corruption_charges "Chen Shui-bian corruption charges").[^297] [^298] [^296]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/%E5%A4%AA%E9%99%BD%E8%8A%B1%E5%AD%B8%E9%81%8B_IMG_5932_%2813822412824%29.jpg/250px-%E5%A4%AA%E9%99%BD%E8%8A%B1%E5%AD%B8%E9%81%8B_IMG_5932_%2813822412824%29.jpg)

Students occupied the Legislative Yuan in protest against a controversial trade agreement with China in March 2014.

The KMT's nominee [Ma Ying-jeou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ma_Ying-jeou "Ma Ying-jeou") won the [2008 presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_Republic_of_China_presidential_election "2008 Republic of China presidential election") on a platform of increased economic growth and better ties with the PRC under a policy of " [mutual non-denial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_non-state-to-state_relations "Special non-state-to-state relations") ".[^295] Under Ma, Taiwan and China opened up direct flights and cargo shipments.[^299] The PRC government even made the atypical decision to not demand that Taiwan be barred from the annual [World Health Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Assembly "World Health Assembly").[^300] Ma also made an official apology for the White Terror.[^301] [^302] However, closer economic ties with China raised concerns about its political consequences.[^303] [^304] In 2014, university students occupied the Legislative Yuan and prevented the ratification of the [Cross-Strait Service Trade Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross-Strait_Service_Trade_Agreement "Cross-Strait Service Trade Agreement") in what became known as the [Sunflower Student Movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunflower_Student_Movement "Sunflower Student Movement"). The movement gave rise to youth-based third parties such as the [New Power Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Power_Party "New Power Party"), and is viewed to have contributed to the DPP's victories in the [2016 presidential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_Taiwanese_presidential_election "2016 Taiwanese presidential election") and [legislative elections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_Taiwanese_legislative_election "2016 Taiwanese legislative election"),[^305] the latter of which resulted in the first DPP legislative majority in Taiwanese history.[^306] In January 2024, [William Lai Ching-te](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lai_Ching-te "Lai Ching-te") of the ruling Democratic Progressive Party won Taiwan's [presidential elections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2024_Taiwanese_presidential_election "2024 Taiwanese presidential election").[^307] However, no party won a majority in the simultaneous Taiwan's [legislative election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2024_Taiwanese_legislative_election "2024 Taiwanese legislative election") for the first time since 2004, meaning 51 seats for the Democratic Progressive Party (DPP), 52 seats for the Kuomintang (KMT), and the [Taiwan People's Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_People%27s_Party "Taiwan People's Party") (TPP) secured eight seats.[^308]

## Geography

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Taiwan_NASA_Terra_MODIS_2022-07-21.jpg/330px-Taiwan_NASA_Terra_MODIS_2022-07-21.jpg)

A satellite image of Taiwan, showing it is mostly mountainous in the east, with gently sloping plains in the west. The Penghu Islands are west of the main island.

The land controlled by the ROC consists of [168 islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Taiwan "List of islands of Taiwan") [^18] with a combined area of 36,193 square kilometers (13,974 sq mi).[^51] [^74] [^14] The main island, known historically as *Formosa*, makes up 99 percent of this area, measuring 35,808 square kilometers (13,826 sq mi) and lying some 180 kilometers (112 mi) across the [Taiwan Strait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_Strait "Taiwan Strait") from the southeastern coast of [mainland China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_China "Mainland China"). The [East China Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_China_Sea "East China Sea") lies to its north, the [Philippine Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippine_Sea "Philippine Sea") to its east, the [Luzon Strait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luzon_Strait "Luzon Strait") directly to its south and the [South China Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_China_Sea "South China Sea") to its southwest. Smaller islands include the [Penghu Islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Penghu "Penghu") in the Taiwan Strait, the [Kinmen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinmen "Kinmen"), [Matsu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matsu_Islands "Matsu Islands") and [Wuqiu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wuqiu,_Kinmen "Wuqiu, Kinmen") islands near the Chinese coast, and some of the [South China Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_China_Sea "South China Sea") islands.

The main island is a tilted [fault block](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fault_block "Fault block"), characterized by the contrast between the eastern two-thirds, consisting mostly of five rugged mountain ranges parallel to the east coast, and the flat to gently rolling plains of the western third, where the majority of Taiwan's population reside. There are several peaks over 3,500 meters, the highest being [Yu Shan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yu_Shan "Yu Shan") at 3,952 m (12,966 ft), making Taiwan the world's [fourth-highest island](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_islands_by_highest_point "List of islands by highest point"). The tectonic boundary that formed these ranges is still active, and the island experiences many earthquakes. There are also many active [submarine volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submarine_volcano "Submarine volcano") in the Taiwan Strait.

Taiwan contains four terrestrial ecoregions: [Jian Nan subtropical evergreen forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jian_Nan_subtropical_evergreen_forests "Jian Nan subtropical evergreen forests"), South China Sea Islands, [South Taiwan monsoon rain forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Taiwan_monsoon_rain_forests "South Taiwan monsoon rain forests"), and [Taiwan subtropical evergreen forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiwan_subtropical_evergreen_forests "Taiwan subtropical evergreen forests").[^309] The eastern mountains are heavily forested and home to a diverse range of wildlife, while land use in the western and northern lowlands is intensive. The country had a 2019 [Forest Landscape Integrity Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index "Forest Landscape Integrity Index") mean score of 6.38/10, ranking it 76th globally out of 172 countries.[^310]

### Climate

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Koppen-Geiger_Map_TWN_present.svg/330px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_TWN_present.svg.png)

Köppen climate classification of Taiwan

Taiwan lies on the [Tropic of Cancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer "Tropic of Cancer"), and its general [climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate "Climate") is marine [tropical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_climate "Tropical climate").[^48] The northern and central regions are subtropical, whereas the south is tropical and the mountainous regions are temperate.[^311] The average rainfall is 2,600 millimeters (100 inches) per year for the island proper; the [rainy season](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asian_rainy_season "East Asian rainy season") is concurrent with the onset of the summer [East Asian Monsoon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asian_Monsoon "East Asian Monsoon") in May and June.[^312] The entire island experiences hot, humid weather from June through September. [Typhoons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Typhoons_in_Taiwan "Typhoons in Taiwan") are most common in July, August and September.[^312] During the winter (November to March), the northeast experiences steady rain, while the central and southern parts of the island are mostly sunny.

Due to [climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_in_Taiwan "Climate change in Taiwan"), the average temperature in Taiwan has risen 1.4 °C (2.5 °F) in the last 100 years, twice the worldwide temperature rise.[^313] The goal of the Taiwanese government is to cut [carbon emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_emissions "Carbon emissions") by 20 percent in 2030 and by 50 percent in 2050, compared to 2005 levels. Carbon emissions increased by 0.92 percent between 2005 and 2016.[^314]

### Geology

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/%E5%A4%A7%E9%9C%B8%E5%B0%96%E5%B1%B1.jpg/250px-%E5%A4%A7%E9%9C%B8%E5%B0%96%E5%B1%B1.jpg)

Mount Dabajian was selected as one of the 100 Peaks of Taiwan.

The island of Taiwan lies in a complex [tectonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tectonics "Tectonics") area between the [Yangtze Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yangtze_Plate "Yangtze Plate") to the west and north, the [Okinawa Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Okinawa_Plate "Okinawa Plate") on the north-east, and the [Philippine Mobile Belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippine_Mobile_Belt "Philippine Mobile Belt") on the east and south. The upper part of the crust on the island is primarily made up of a series of [terranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrane "Terrane"), mostly old [island arcs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Island_arc "Island arc") which have been forced together by the collision of the forerunners of the [Eurasian Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_Plate "Eurasian Plate") and the [Philippine Sea Plate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate "Philippine Sea Plate"). These have been further uplifted as a result of the detachment of a portion of the Eurasian Plate as it was [subducted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subduction "Subduction") beneath remnants of the Philippine Sea Plate, a process which left the crust under Taiwan more buoyant.[^315]

The east and south of Taiwan are a complex system of belts formed by, and part of the zone of, active collision between the North Luzon Trough portion of the [Luzon Volcanic Arc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luzon_Volcanic_Arc "Luzon Volcanic Arc") and South China, where accreted portions of the Luzon Arc and Luzon forearc form the [eastern Coastal Range](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haian_Range "Haian Range") and parallel inland [Longitudinal Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huatung_Valley "Huatung Valley") of Taiwan, respectively.[^316]

The major seismic faults in Taiwan correspond to the various suture zones between the various terranes. These have produced major quakes. On 21 September 1999, a 7.3 quake known as the " [921 earthquake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1999_Jiji_\(Chichi\)_earthquake "1999 Jiji (Chichi) earthquake") " killed more than 2,400 people. The [seismic hazard map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seismic_hazard_map "Seismic hazard map") for Taiwan by the [USGS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey "United States Geological Survey") shows 9/10 of the island at the most hazardous rating.[^317]

## Government and politics

### Government

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/%E4%B8%96%E7%95%8C%E6%9C%80%E7%BE%8E%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E5%BA%9C.jpg/250px-%E4%B8%96%E7%95%8C%E6%9C%80%E7%BE%8E%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E5%BA%9C.jpg)

Taiwan's popularly elected president resides in the Presidential Office Building, Taipei, originally built in the Japanese era for colonial governors.

The government of the Republic of China was founded on the 1947 [Constitution of the ROC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_China "Constitution of the Republic of China") and its [Three Principles of the People](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three_Principles_of_the_People "Three Principles of the People"), which states that the ROC "shall be a democratic republic of the people, to be governed by the people and for the people".[^318] It underwent significant revisions in the 1990s, known collectively as the Additional Articles. The government is divided into five branches (*Yuan*): the Executive Yuan (cabinet), the [Legislative Yuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legislative_Yuan "Legislative Yuan") (Congress or Parliament), the [Judicial Yuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judicial_Yuan "Judicial Yuan"), the [Control Yuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Control_Yuan "Control Yuan") (audit agency), and the [Examination Yuan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Examination_Yuan "Examination Yuan") (civil service examination agency).

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/%E8%B3%B4%E6%B8%85%E5%BE%B7%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-%E8%B3%B4%E6%B8%85%E5%BE%B7%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1_%28cropped%29.jpg)

Lai Ching-te, President of the Republic of China

The [head of state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head_of_state "Head of state") and [commander-in-chief](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commander-in-chief "Commander-in-chief") of the armed forces is the [president](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_Republic_of_China "President of the Republic of China"), who is elected by popular vote for a maximum of 2 four-year terms on the same ticket as the vice-president. The president appoints the members of the Executive Yuan as their cabinet, including a [premier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Premier_of_the_Republic_of_China "Premier of the Republic of China"), who is officially the President of the Executive Yuan; members are responsible for policy and administration.[^318]

The main [legislative body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legislature "Legislature") is the [unicameral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unicameralism "Unicameralism") Legislative Yuan with 113 seats. Seventy-three are elected by popular vote from single-member constituencies; thirty-four are elected based on the proportion of nationwide votes received by participating political parties in a separate party list ballot; and six are elected from two three-member aboriginal constituencies. Members serve four-year terms. Originally, the unicameral National Assembly, as a standing [constitutional convention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_convention_\(political_meeting\) "Constitutional convention (political meeting)") and [electoral college](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electoral_college "Electoral college"), held some parliamentary functions, but the [National Assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Assembly_\(Republic_of_China\) "National Assembly (Republic of China)") was abolished in 2005 with the power of constitutional amendments handed over to the Legislative Yuan and all eligible voters of the Republic via referendums.[^318] [^319]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Premier_Cho_Jung-tai_20240820.jpg/250px-Premier_Cho_Jung-tai_20240820.jpg)

Cho Jung-tai, Premier of the Republic of China

The premier is selected by the president without the need for approval from the legislature, and neither the president nor the premier wields veto power.[^318] Historically, the ROC has been dominated by strongman single party politics. This legacy has resulted in executive powers currently being concentrated in the office of the president rather than the premier.[^320]

The Judicial Yuan is the highest [judicial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judiciary "Judiciary") organ. It interprets the constitution and other laws and decrees, judges administrative suits, and disciplines public functionaries. The president and vice-president of the Judicial Yuan and additional thirteen justices form the Constitutional Court, which was established in 1993 to resolve constitutional disputes, regulate the activities of political parties and accelerate the democratization process. The Constitutional Court was formerly called the Council of Grand Justices.[^321] They are nominated and appointed by the president, with the consent of the Legislative Yuan. The highest court, the [Supreme Court](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_Republic_of_China "Supreme Court of the Republic of China"), consists of a number of civil and criminal divisions, each of which is formed by a presiding judge and four associate judges, all appointed for life. The right to a fair public trial is protected by law and respected in practice. There is no [trial by jury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jury_trial "Jury trial"), however many cases are presided over by multiple judges.[^318]

The Control Yuan is a watchdog agency that monitors the actions of the executive. It can be considered a standing [commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_agency "Government agency") for administrative inquiry, like the [Court of Auditors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Court_of_Auditors "European Court of Auditors") of the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") or the [Government Accountability Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office "Government Accountability Office") of the United States.[^318] It is also responsible for the [National Human Rights Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Human_Rights_Commission_\(Taiwan\) "National Human Rights Commission (Taiwan)").

The Examination Yuan is in charge of validating the qualification of civil servants. It is based on the [imperial examination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperial_examination "Imperial examination") system used in dynastic China. It can be compared to the [European Personnel Selection Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Personnel_Selection_Office "European Personnel Selection Office") of the European Union or the [Office of Personnel Management](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_Personnel_Management "Office of Personnel Management") of the United States.[^318] It was downsized in 2019, and there have been calls for its abolition.[^322] [^323]

### Constitution

The constitution was drafted by the KMT while the ROC still governed the Chinese mainland.[^324] Political reforms beginning in the late 1970s resulted in the end of martial law in 1987, and Taiwan transformed into a multiparty democracy in the early 1990s. The constitutional basis for this transition to democracy was gradually laid in the [Additional Articles of the Constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additional_Articles_of_the_Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_China "Additional Articles of the Constitution of the Republic of China"). These articles suspended portions of the Constitution designed for the governance of mainland China and replacing them with articles adapted for the governance of and guaranteeing the political rights of residents of the Taiwan Area, as defined in the [Cross-Strait Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross-Strait_Act "Cross-Strait Act").[^325]
